有钱人的妻子听说后便立即跑回去，把这一切如实告诉了她丈夫。有钱人说：“我真该把自己撕碎！我要早知道会这样就好了！那个赶路人也来我们家的，想要在这里借宿一夜，可我把他打发走了。”他的妻子说：“快！骑上你的马。你还能赶上他，然后要让他也满足你的三个愿望。” When the rich man’s wife heard this, she ran back in haste and told her husband how it had happened.The man said, “I could tear myself to pieces!If I had but known that!That traveler came to our house too, and wanted to sleep here, and I sent him away.”“Quick!” said his wife, “get on your horse.You can still catch the man up, and then you must ask to have three wishes granted to you.”
有钱人听从了妻子的建议，他骑上马飞奔起来，很快就追上了上帝。富人温柔又客气地对上帝说话，恳求他不要怪自己没立即请他进屋。他当时正在寻找前门的钥匙，而上帝那会儿却走开了，他恳请上帝如果要返回原路的话，务必到他家去住一夜。上帝说：“好的，如果我再回去的话，我会去的。”于是，有钱人问上帝，他是不是也能像邻居那样提出三个愿望。上帝说：“可以。”他可以提出愿望，可是那样对他没什么好处，上帝劝他最好不提。可有钱人却心想，要是愿望能够得到满足的话，他可要提点要求让自己的生活更加幸福。于是上帝对他说：“那你骑马回家吧，你的三个愿望我都会满足的。” The rich man followed the good counsel and galloped away on his horse, and soon came up with the Lord.He spoke to him softly and pleasantly, and begged him not to take it amiss that he had not let him in directly; he was looking for the frontdoor key, and in the meantime the stranger had gone away, if he returned the same way he must come and stay with him.“Yes,” said the Lord; “if I ever come back again, I will do so.”Then the rich man asked if might not wish for three things too, as his neighbor had done?“Yes,” said the Lord, he might, but it would not be to his advantage, and he had better not wish for anything; but the rich man thought that he could easily ask for something which would add to his happiness, if he only knew that it would be granted.So the Lord said to him, “Ride home, then, and three wishes which you shall form, shall be fulfilled.”
有钱人达到了目的，便骑马回家，并开始考虑自己要提什么愿望。他正想着的时候，不小心弄掉了马缰绳，马开始乱蹦乱跳，以至于他没办法继续思考，都完全不能集中注意力了。他拍拍马的脖子，说道：“莉萨，老实点。”可是，那马又玩起了新花样。最后，他终于忍无可忍，不耐烦地大喊道：“我真希望扭断你的脖子！”他话刚出口，那马就忽然倒了下去，已经死了，再也动弹不得。就这样，他的第一个愿望得到了满足。由于他生性吝啬，不甘心把那副马鞍留在那里。于是，他卸下马鞍扛到背上，现在只得步行回家了。他说道：“我还有两个愿望。”想到这里心里便好受多了。 The rich man had now gained what he wanted, so he rode home, and began to consider what he should wish for.As he was thus thinking he let the bridle fall, and the horse began to caper about, so that he was continually disturbed in his meditations, and could not collect his thoughts at all.He patted its neck, and said, “Gently, Lisa,” but the horse only began new tricks.Then at last he was angry, and cried quite impatiently, “I wish your neck was broken!”Directly he had said the words, down the horse fell on the ground, and there it lay dead and never moved again.And thus was his first wish fulfilled.As he was miserly by nature, he did not like to leave the harness lying there; so he cut it off, and put it on his back; and now he had to go on foot.“I have still two wishes left,” said he, and comforted himself with that thought.
他缓步行走在沙地上面，中午的太阳火辣辣地照射着，他变得烦躁又愤怒。马鞍还弄疼了他的后背，他想不出该提出点什么愿望。他自言自语道：“即使我许愿得到了世界上所有的财宝，我以后还是会想要其它各样东西的，我知道会这样。我要想出一个好办法来，把所有的东西都要来，以免以后又想要。”于是，他叹了一口气，说：“啊，我要是是一名也有三次机会实现愿望的巴伐利亚州农夫就好了，那我就清楚自己要什么了：首先我要许多的啤酒；其次我要喝不完的啤酒；最后我还要一桶啤酒。” And now as he was walking slowly through the sand, and the sun was burning hot at noonday, he grew quite hot-tempered and angry.The saddle hurt his back, and he had not yet any idea what to wish for.“If I were to wish for all the riches and treasures in the world,” said he to himself, “I should still to think of all kinds of other things later on, I know that, beforehand.But I will manage so that there is nothing at all left me to wish for afterwards.”Then he sighed and said, “Ah, if I were but that Bavarian peasant, who likewise had three wishes granted to him, and knew quite well what to do, and in the first place wished for a great deal of beer, and in the second for as much beer as he was able to drink, and in the third for a barrel of beer into the bargain.”
他很多次都认为自己有主意了，可过一会儿又觉得对他来说还是太少了。这时，他忽然想到妻子的生活多么惬意呀，因为她正呆在家中凉爽的屋子里享受。这让他很恼怒，不假思索地就说道：“我真希望她坐在这马鞍上，再也下不来，那样我也不用背着它赶路了。”他话音刚落，马鞍忽然就从他背上消失了，他意识到自己的第二个愿望也得到了满足。他觉得更加热了。他开始跑起来，想赶紧回到家中自己的屋子里，去想一想他最后的愿望，他想要一个足够大的东西。可是，当他赶回家打开门后，看到妻子正坐在屋中央的马鞍上，边哭喊边抱怨，无论如何就是下不来。于是，他说道：“忍忍吧，我要许愿把全世界的财宝都给你，就坐在那里吧。”而她却骂他是傻瓜，说道：“要是我只能坐在这马鞍上，全世界的财宝又能给我带来什么好处呢？是你许愿让我坐在这上面，那你就得帮我摆脱它。”因此，不管他情愿不情愿，他只得提出第三个愿望，那就是让他的妻子从马鞍上下来，他的愿望又立即实现了。就这样，有钱人什么也没得到，他只是恼怒、疲倦、辱骂，还损失了一匹马。而穷苦人却过上了都幸福恬静的生活，夫妻俩一生都笃信上帝。 Many a time he thought he had found it, but then it seemed to him to be, after all, too little.Then it came into his mind, what an easy life his wife had, for she stayed at home in a cool room and enjoyed herself.This really did vex him, and before he was aware, he said, “I just wish she was sitting there on this saddle, and could not get off it, instead of my having to drag it along on my back.”And as the last word was spoken, the saddle disappeared from his back, and he saw that his second wish had been fulfilled.Then he really did feel warm.He began to run and wanted to be quite alone in his own room at home, to think of something really large for his last wish.But when he arrived there and opened the parlour door, he saw his wife sitting in the middle of the room on the saddle, crying and complaining, and quite unable to get off it.So he said, “Do bear it, and I will wish for all the riches on earth for thee, only stay where thou art.”She, however, called him a fool, and said, “What good will all the riches on earth do me, if I am to sit on this saddle?Thou hast wished me on it, so thou must help me off.”So whether he would or not, he was forced to let his third wish be that she should be quit of the saddle, and able to get off it, and immediately the wish was fulfilled.So he got nothing by it but vexation, trouble, abuse, and the loss of his horse; but the poor people lived happily, quietly, and piously until their happy death.
出色的猎人 The Skilful Huntsman
从前有个年轻人，他学会了锁匠活，便对父亲说，他现在想去外面的世界闯一闯，碰碰运气。“很好，”父亲说道，“我非常同意你的想法。”父亲还给了他一些钱当路费。于是他便四处飘荡，想找点活儿干。过了一段时间，他决定不再去找锁匠活了，因为他已经不再喜欢这一行了，相反喜欢上了狩猎。有一天他在路上闲逛，遇到了一位身穿绿色衣服的猎人，猎人问他从什么地方来的，又要到什么地方去。小伙子回答说自己是个锁匠学徒，可是现在不想做锁匠了，很喜欢打猎，不知猎人肯不肯教他打猎。“噢，可以，”猎人说，“只要你肯跟我走。”于是年轻人就跟着他去了，跟了猎人几年，从他那儿学会了狩猎的技术。学会狩猎后，小伙子决定去别处闯闯。临别之时，猎人并没有给他任何报酬，而是送了他一支气枪，这支枪很不一般，只要用它射击目标物，肯定是百发百中。小伙子出发之后，来到一片非常非常大的森林，他走了整整一天都没有走到尽头。天黑的时候，他爬上了一棵大树，这样可以避免野兽的侵袭。快到午夜之时，他仿佛看见远处闪烁着一点微光。于是他便穿过树枝向下往光亮处看去，弄清楚它的位置。但他还是先把帽子摘下来，朝着亮光的方向扔下去，这样他从树上下来后，就可以把帽子作为路标向前走。随后，他下了树，走到帽子那里把它戴上，然后继续向前走去。他越往前走，亮光就变得越大，等他走近一看，那原来是一个硕大的火堆，旁边坐着三个巨人，他们正用炙叉叉着一头牛在火上烤呢。这时候，其中的一个巨人说道：“我得尝尝肉是不是马上就可以吃了。”于是他便撕了一块下来，肉还没到嘴边，就被猎人一枪射飞了。“嘿呀，风还把肉从我手中刮跑啦！”于是他便又撕了一块。谁知他刚张嘴要咬，肉又被猎人一枪射飞了。这时巨人便打了坐在旁边的同伴一个耳光，并厉声怒喝道：“你干嘛抢走我的肉？”“我没有抢你的肉啊，”旁边的巨人说道，“一定是哪位神枪手把你的肉给射飞了。”巨人又撕了一块肉下来，但是还没拿稳，就让猎人给一枪射飞了。这时巨人说：“能把人嘴边的食物射飞，那一定是个顶级射手，这样的高手对我们会有用的。”于是他便大声喊道：“出来吧神枪手，出来和我们一起坐到火边吃东西，我们不会伤害你的，你倘若不出来，我们可要动手抓你啦，那你就完喽！”听了这些话，小伙子便走到他们跟前，告诉他们说自己是一个训练有素的猎手，无论用猎枪瞄准什么，都是百发百中。随后，巨人们对小伙子说，只要他肯跟着他们，就一定能得到好处的，他们还说森林外边有一大片湖泊，湖的那边耸立着一座塔楼，塔楼里关着一位漂亮的公主，他们非常想把公主抢走。“好的，”小伙子说，“我马上就去把她给你们抢过来。”接着巨人们又说：“可是还有一件事，塔里有一只小狗，只要一有人靠近，它就汪汪直叫，而它一叫，宫里的人就都醒了，所以我们根本进不去，你能不能去把那只狗射死啊？”“能，”小伙子说，“这对我来说简直是小菜一碟。”随后他上了一艘船，划到了湖对岸。刚一上岸，小狗便跑了过来张嘴要叫，这时猎人拿出气枪，将狗射死了。巨人看到这一场景，非常高兴，心里想公主这下肯定是他们的了，但是猎人却想先了解事情的究竟，于是便让巨人呆在塔外，说要等他叫他们，他们才能进来。随后，他走进城堡，里面一片寂静，大家都在睡觉。当他打开第一间屋子的门时，看到墙上挂着一把纯银制成的剑，上面还镶着一颗金星和国王的名字，旁边的桌上放着一封密封的信，他打开信一看，只见上面写着，如果谁拿到这把剑，就可以战胜一切。于是他从墙上取下剑，挂在身侧，继续向前走。随后他来到公主睡觉的房间，发现公主是那么漂亮，他屏住呼吸站在那里，一动不动地看着她。他心里想：“我怎么能将一个无辜的女孩交到野蛮而邪恶的巨人手里呢？”他继续环视，发现在床下放着一双拖鞋，右边的一只绣着她父亲的名字和一颗星星，左边的一只绣着她自己的名字和一颗星星。她还围着一条特大的镶金丝绸围巾，围巾右边绣着她父亲的名字，左边绣着她自己的名字，所有的字母都是金灿灿的。于是猎人便拿出一把剪刀，将右边的一片剪下来放到自己的背包中，然后又收起绣着国王名字的右边那只拖鞋，同样塞进了背包里。现在公主依然躺在那里熟睡着，身子全都裹在睡衣里，于是小伙子便从睡衣上剪下一小块，一并塞进背包里，但是他做这一切并没有碰公主一下。之后，他便走了，留下安然熟睡的公主，等他回到门口，三个巨人依旧站在外面等着，一心想着他会把公主带来。而他却大声叫他们进去，还说公主已经控制在他们的手中了，只是他给他们开不了门，好在门上有个洞，他们必须得钻洞进去了。于是，第一个巨人走到洞口，猎人将他的头发挽在手上，拽住他的头往里拉，接着一刀将他的头砍了下来，然后将整个身子拽了进去。他招呼第二个巨人过来，用同样的方法砍了他的头，随后又将第三个也杀了。他把美丽的公主从敌人的手中解救出来，非常开心，于是便将巨人们的舌头割下来放进了自己的背包。这时他心想：“我要回家去见父亲了，让他知道我干的这一切，之后我就去环游世界，上帝赐给我的福气一定会降临到我身上的。” THERE was once a young fellow who had learnt the trade of locksmith, and told his father he would now go out into the world and seek his fortune.“Very well,” said the father, “I am quite content with that,” and gave him some money for his journey.So he travelled about and looked for work.After a time he resolved not to follow the trade of locksmith any more, for he no longer liked it, but he took a fancy for hunting.Then there met him in his rambles a huntsman dressed in green, who asked whence he came and whither he was going.The youth said he was a locksmith’s apprentice, but that the trade no longer pleased him, and he had a liking for huntsmanship, would he teach it to him?“Oh, yes,” said the huntsman, “if thou wilt go with me.”Then the young fellow went with him, bound himself to him for some years, and learnt the art of hunting.After this he wished to try his luck elsewhere, and the huntsman gave him nothing in the way of payment but an air-gun, which had, however, this property, that it hit its mark without fail whenever he shot with it.Then he set out and found himself in a very large forest, which he could not get to the end of in one day.When evening came he seated himself in a high tree in order to escape from the wild beasts.Towards midnight, it seemed to him as if a tiny little light glimmered in the distance.Then he looked down through the branches towards it, and kept well in his mind where it was.But in the first place he took off his hat and threw it down in the direction of the light, so that he might go to the hat as a mark when he had descended.Then he got down and went to his hat, put it on again and went straight forwards.The farther he went, the larger the light grew, and when he got close to it he saw that it was an enormous fire, and that three giants were sitting by it, who had an ox on the spit, and were roasting it.Presently one of them said, “I must just taste if the meat will soon be fit to eat,” and pulled a piece off, and was about to put it in his mouth when the huntsman shot it out of his hand.“Well, really,” said the giant, “if the wind has not blown the bit out of my hand!” and helped himself to another.But when he was just about to bite into it, the huntsman again shot it away from him.On this the giant gave the one who was sitting next him a box on the ear, and cried angrily, Why art thou snatching my piece away from me?”“I have not snatched it away,” said the other, “a sharpshooter must have shot it away from thee.”The giant took another piece, but could not, however, keep it in his hand, for the huntsman shot it out.Then the giant said, “That must be a good shot to shoot the bit out of one’s very mouth, such an one would be useful to us.”And he cried aloud, “Come here, thou sharpshooter, seat thyself at the fire beside us and eat thy fill, we will not hurt thee; but if thou wilt not come, and we have to bring thee by force, thou art a lost man!”On this the youth went up to them and told them he was a skilled huntsman, and that whatever he aimed at with his gun, he was certain to hit.Then they said if he would go with them he should be well treated, and they told him that outside the forest there was a great lake, behind which stood a tower, and in the tower was imprisoned a lovely princess, whom they wished very much to carry off.“Yes,” said he, “I will soon get her for you.”Then they added, “But there is still something else, there is a tiny little dog, which begins to bark directly any one goes near, and as soon as it barks every one in the royal palace wakens up, and for this reason we cannot get there; canst thou undertake to shoot it dead?”“Yes,” said he, “that will be a little bit of fun for me.”After this he got into a boat and rowed over the lake, and as soon as he landed, the little dog came running out, and was about to bark, but the huntsman took his air-gun and shot it dead.When the giants saw that, they rejoiced, and thought they already had the King’s daughter safe, but the huntsman wished first to see how matters stood, and told them that they must stay outside until he called them.Then he went into the castle, and all was perfectly quiet within, and every one was asleep.When he opened the door of the first room, a sword was hanging on the wall which was made of pure silver, and there was a golden star on it, and the name of the King, and on a table near it lay a sealed letter which he broke open, and inside it was written that whosoever had the sword could kill everything which opposed him.So he took the sword from the wall, hung it at his side and went onwards: then he entered the room where the King’s daughter was lying sleeping, and she was so beautiful that he stood still and, holding his breath, looked at her.He thought to himself, “How can I give an innocent maiden into the power of the wild giants, who have evil in their minds?”He looked about further, and under the bed stood a pair of slippers, on the right one was her father’s name with a star, and on the left her own name with a star.She wore also a great neck-kerchief of silk embroidered with gold, and on the right side was her father’s name, and on the left her own, all in golden letters.Then the huntsman took a pair of scissors and cut the right corner off, and put it in his knapsack, and then he also took the right slipper with the King’s name, and thrust that in.Now the maiden still lay sleeping, and she was quite sewn into her night-dress, and he cut a morsel from this also, and thrust it in with the rest, but he did all without touching her.Then he went forth and left her lying asleep undisturbed, and when he came to the gate again, the giants were still standing outside waiting for him, and expecting that he was bringing the princess.But he cried to them that they were to come in, for the maiden was already in their power, that he could not open the gate to them, but there was a hole through which they must creep.Then the first approached, and the huntsman wound the giant’s hair round his hand, pulled the head in, and cut it off at one stroke with his sword, and then drew the rest of him in.He called to the second and cut his head off likewise, and then he killed the third also, and he was well pleased that he had freed the beautiful maiden from her enemies, and he cut out their tongues and put them in his knapsack.Then thought he, “I will go home to my father and let him see what I have already done, and afterwards I will travel about the world; the luck which God is pleased to grant me will easily find me.”
城堡里的国王醒来之后，发现了地上躺着三个巨人的尸体。于是他便走进女儿的房间，把叫醒她后，问她是谁杀了这三个巨人。公主回答说：“亲爱的父王，我不知道，我一直在睡觉呢。”说完便起身想穿鞋下床，谁知右脚的一只拖鞋不见了，再看看围巾，右边被剪掉了一片，最后瞅瞅自己的睡衣，居然也少了一块。国王召集了宫廷里所有的人，包括卫兵和其他在场的人，问他们是谁解救了自己的女儿，而且杀死了三个巨人。这时他手下的一个相貌丑陋的独眼将军站出来说是他干的。于是国王便说，既然他做了这么一件了不起的事，那么就把女儿嫁给他吧。但是公主却说：“亲爱的父王，要我嫁给这样一个人，我宁愿去外面流浪，哪怕是走到天涯海角。”但是国王却说，如果她不肯嫁给这个将军，那么她就必须脱去华丽的衣服，穿上贫民的粗布衣，然后离开皇宫，他还说要女儿找一个陶工，在那里学卖陶器。公主却真的脱下了她华丽的衣服，然后来到一个陶器坊，赊了一堆陶器摆起了地摊，她还向那陶工承诺说，如果天黑前她将陶器卖完了，她就把钱还给他。于是国王便命令公主到一个街角去摆摊卖陶器，他还安排了一些农民赶着车从那个摊儿上碾过去，这样所有的陶器都将变成碎片。因此，当公主在街上摆起摊儿来的时候，几辆车便驶了过来，将她的陶器全部碾成了碎片。她哭着说道：“天啊，这下我该怎么还那位陶工的钱呢？”尽管国王是想通过这种方法迫使她嫁给那位将军，她却并没有答应，而是又跑到陶工那里，问他是否能再次赊货给她。陶工说不行，要她先还清上一次的钱。随后她便回到国王那里，放声痛哭着说自己要去外边的世界流浪。这时国王说道：“我会叫人在外边的树林里给你盖一个小房子，你要一辈子都呆在里面，给所有过往的人烧饭，但是你不许收钱。”小屋盖好后，就在门上挂了个牌子，上面写着：“今日免费，明日收钱。”公主便长时间呆在那里，外边很快就传开了，说森林里住着一位姑娘，免费给路人烧饭，她的门上就这样写着呢。猎人也听说了这件事，心里想着：“对我来说，这倒是不错啊。我正穷得没饭吃呢。”于是他便拿起气枪，背起背包，包里还装着先前他从皇宫拿出来的所有证物，他来到森林里，找到了挂着牌子的小屋，牌子上果真写着：“今日免费，明日收钱。”他身上挂着曾经砍掉过三个巨人脑袋的宝剑，来到小屋里，向姑娘点了些吃的。他见到这么一位美丽的姑娘非常开心，而公主也确实长得像画上画得一样漂亮。她问猎人是从哪儿来的，要上哪儿去。猎人说：“我在周游世界。”这时公主便问他是哪儿弄来的这把宝剑，因为上面确实刻着自己父亲的名字。他却反问她是不是公主。“是的。”她回答说。“就是用这把剑，”他说，“我砍掉了三个巨人的头。”说着他便从背包里拿出他们的舌头，以示证明。随后他又给她看了那只拖鞋、那片围巾和那块睡衣。公主喜出望外，说他才是那个正真救了自己的男子。于是，他们俩一起去见老国王，把他请到小屋里来，接着公主将他带到自己的房间，告诉他猎人才是那个真正将自己从巨人手中救出来的人。老国王看了所有的证物之后，便不再怀疑了，他说知道了一切真相他很开心，而猎人也应该娶公主做妻子，听了国王的话，公主开心极了。随后，公主给猎人穿上了盛装，他看上去仿佛是他国的贵族一般。国王也吩咐人准备了宴席。大家都坐上席后，独眼将军坐在公主的左边，猎人坐在公主的右边，那将军还以为猎人是他国来访的贵族。酒宴之后，国王对独眼将军说，他要给他出题考考他。题目是：“假如有人说自己杀死了三个巨人，而当他被问起巨人的舌头哪儿去了的时候，他却说他得去看看，结果一看嘴里没有舌头，那这是怎么回事？”将军说：“那就是巨人没有舌头。”“胡说。”国王说，“凡是动物都有舌头。”接着他又问，要是有人这样回答了，那么他该当何罪。将军回答说：“应将他碎尸万段。”国王便告诉他说，他已经说出了自己的判决。将军被关进了监狱，然后被剁成了四块，而公主却嫁给了猎人。不久后，猎人接来了他的父母，两位老人和儿子一起过着幸福快乐的生活。老国王去世后，猎人便继承了王位。 But when the King in the castle awoke, he saw the three giants lying there dead.So he went into the sleeping-room of his daughter, awoke her, and asked who could have killed the giants?Then said she, “Dear father, I know not, I have been asleep.”But when she arose and would have put on her slippers, the right one was gone, and when she looked at her neck-kerchief it was cut, and the right corner was missing, and when she looked at her night-dress a piece was cut out of it.The King summoned his whole court together, soldiers and every one else who was there, and asked who had set his daughter at liberty, and killed the giants?Now it happened that he had a captain, who was one-eyed and a hideous man, and he said that he had done it.Then the old King said that as he had accomplished this, he should marry his daughter.But the maiden said, “Rather than marry him, dear father, I will go away into the world as far as my legs can carry me.”But the King said that if she would not marry him she should take off her royal garments and wear peasant’s clothing, and go forth, and that she should go to a potter, and begin a trade in earthen vessels.So she put off her royal apparel, and went to a potter and borrowed crockery enough for a stall, and she promised him also that if she had sold it by the evening, she would pay for it.Then the King said she was to seat herself in a corner with it and sell it, and he arranged with some peasants to drive over it with their carts, so that everything should be broken into a thousand pieces.When therefore the King’s daughter had placed her stall in the street, by came the carts, and broke all she had into tiny fragments.She began to weep and said, “Alas, how shall I ever pay for the pots now?”The King had, however, wished by this to force her to marry the captain; but instead of that, she again went to the potter, and asked him if he would lend to her once more.He said, “No,” she must first pay for the things she had already had.Then she went to her father and cried and lamented, and said she would go forth into the world.Then said he, “I will have a little hut built for thee in the forest outside, and in it thou shalt stay all thy life long and cook for every one, but thou shalt take no money for it.”When the hut was ready, a sign was hung on the door whereon was written, “To-day given, to-morrow sold.”There she remained a long time, and it was rumored about the world that a maiden was there who cooked without asking for payment, and that this was set forth on a sign outside her door.The huntsman heard it likewise, and thought to himself, “That would suit thee.Thou art poor, and hast no money.”So he took his air-gun and his knapsack, wherein all the things which he had formerly carried away with him from the castle as tokens of his truthfulness were still lying, and went into the forest, and found the hut with the sign, “To-day given, to-morrow sold.”He had put on the sword with which he had cut off the heads of the three giants, and thus entered the hut, and ordered something to eat to be given to him.He was charmed with the beautiful maiden, who was indeed as lovely as any picture.She asked him whence he came and whither he was going, and he said, “I am roaming about the world.”Then she asked him where he had got the sword, for that truly her father’s name was on it.He asked her if she were the King’s daughter.“Yes,” answered she.“With this sword,” said he, “did I cut off the heads of three giants.”And he took their tongues out of his knapsack in proof.Then he also showed her the slipper, and the corner of the neck-kerchief, and the bit of the night-dress.Hereupon she was overjoyed, and said that he was the one who had delivered her.On this they went together to the old King, and fetched him to the hut, and she led him into her room, and told him that the huntsman was the man who had really set her free from the giants.And when the aged King saw all the proofs of this, he could no longer doubt, and said that he was very glad he knew how everything had happened, and that the huntsman should have her to wife, on which the maiden was glad at heart.Then she dressed the huntsman as if he were a foreign lord, and the King ordered a feast to be prepared.When they went to table, the captain sat on the left side of the King’s daughter, but the huntsman was on the right, and the captain thought he was a foreign lord who had come on a visit.When they had eaten and drunk, the old King said to the captain that he would set before him something which he must guess.“Supposing any one said that he had killed the three giants and he were asked where the giants’ tongues were, and he were forced to go and look, and there were none in their heads, how could that happen?”The captain said, “Then they cannot have had any.”“Not so,” said the King.“Every animal has a tongue,” and then he likewise asked what any one would deserve who made such an answer.The captain replied, “He ought to be torn in pieces.”Then the King said he had pronounced his own sentence, and the captain was put in prison and then torn in four pieces; but the King’s daughter was married to the huntsman.After this he brought his father and mother, and they lived with their son in happiness, and after the death of the old King he received the kingdom.
傻大胆学害怕 The Story of the Youth Who Went Forth to Learn What Fear Was
一位父亲有两个儿子，大儿子既聪明伶俐又通情达理，什么都会做，可二儿子不仅笨头笨脑，还什么都不学，也什么都不会，人们看到他时总会说：“这个家伙可够让他爸爸操心的了！”有家务活要做的时候，总是大儿子去做；可是，天色已晚或深夜的时候父亲让他去取东西，还得经过墓地或者其他比较黑的地方的话，他就会回答说：“噢，不，爸爸，我不去那里，我会吓得发抖的。”因为他害怕。人们晚上围着火炉讲故事，讲到让人毛骨悚然的地方，有时听众便会说：“啊，吓得我们都发抖了！”二儿子坐在角落里，听着大家讲话，却不知道他们说的是什么意思。“他们总是说‘吓得我发抖，吓得我发抖！’可我却不知道什么是发抖。”他心里暗想，“这一定也是我不懂的一门技术。” A certain father had two sons, the elder of whom was smart and sensible, and could do everything, but the younger was stupid and could neither learn nor understand anything, and when people saw him they said, “There’s a fellow who will give his father some trouble!”When anything had to be done, it was always the elder who was forced to do it; but if his father bade him fetch anything when it was late, or in the night-time, and the way led through the churchyard, or any other dismal place, he answered “Oh, no, father, I’ll not go there, it makes me shudder!” for he was afraid.Or when stories were told by the fire at night which made the flesh creep, the listeners sometimes said “Oh, it makes us shudder!”The younger sat in a corner and listened with the rest of them, and could not imagine what they could mean.“They are always saying ‘it makes me shudder, it makes me shudder!’It does not make me shudder,” thought he.“That, too, must be an art of which I understand nothing.”
一天，父亲对他说：“你在墙角那里给我听清楚，你已经长得又高又壮了，必须去学点本事谋生了。看看你哥哥多能干，你却连自食其力都做不到。”他答道：“唉，爸爸，我倒是很想去学点本事啊。要是能行的话，我就去学学怎样发抖吧。我对它还一窍不通呢。”大儿子听到这番话后笑起来，他心想：“我的天啊，我弟弟真是个傻瓜呀！他一辈子也做不好任何事。他要是有能力也早该显露出来了。” Now it came to pass that his father said to him one day “Hearken to me, thou fellow in the corner there, thou art growing tall and strong, and thou too must learn something by which thou canst earn thy living.Look how thy brother works, but thou dost not even earn thy salt.”“Well, father,” he replied, “I am quite willing to learn something indeed, if it could but be managed, I should like to learn how to shudder.I don’t understand that at all yet.”The elder brother smiled when he heard that, and thought to himself, “Good God, what a blockhead that brother of mine is!He will never be good for anything as long as he lives.He who wants to be a sickle must bend himself betimes.”
父亲叹了一口气，回答说：“你很快就会知道什么是发抖，可那养活不了你啊。” The father sighed, and answered him “thou shalt soon learn what it is to shudder, but thou wilt not earn thy bread by that.”
不久，家里来了一位教堂的司事，父亲向他诉苦，告诉他小儿子各个方面都很差劲儿，什么也不懂，什么也学不会。他说：“你想想看，我问他以后怎么来养活自己，他竟然说想去学发抖。”司事答道：“如果只是那样的话，我可以教会他发抖。让他来找我吧，我很快就会教好他的。”父亲很高兴地答应了，因为他心想：“这样倒会教育教育这孩子了。”因此，司事带他回家了，他让男孩去敲钟。一两天后，司事在半夜叫醒他，让他起床到钟楼上去敲钟。司事心想：“你很快就知道什么是发抖了。”他先于男孩悄悄地来到了那里。当男孩来到楼顶，刚转身要去抓钟绳的时候，看到在回音洞对面的楼梯上有一个白色的身影。他喊道：“谁在那里？”可那个身影一言不发，也不动弹。男孩又喊道：“回答我，不然就赶紧走，你晚上在这里干什么。” Soon after this the sexton came to the house on a visit, and the father bewailed his trouble, and told him how his younger son was so backward in every respect that he knew nothing and learnt nothing.“Just think,” said he, “when I asked him how he was going to earn his bread, he actually wanted to learn to shudder.”“If that be all,” replied the sexton, “he can learn that with me.Send him to me, and I will soon polish him.”The father was glad to do it, for he thought, “It will train the boy a little.”The sexton therefore took him into his house, and he had to ring the bell.After a day or two, the sexton awoke him at midnight, and bade him arise and go up into the church tower and ring the bell.“Thou shalt soon learn what shuddering is,” thought he, and secretly went there before him; and when the boy was at the top of the tower and turned round, and was just going to take hold of the bell rope, he saw a white figure standing on the stairs opposite the sounding hole.“Who is there?” cried he, but the figure made no reply, and did not move or stir.“Give an answer,” cried the boy, “or take thy self off, thou hast no business here at night.”
然而，司事还是一动不动，男孩以为他是鬼。男孩再次喊道：“你到底想做什么？如果你是个好人，就说话，不然我就把你扔下台阶！”司事心想：“他不会像他说的那样做的。”便还是一言不发，纹丝不动地站在那里，如同是一尊石像。于是男孩第三次喊他，可依旧毫无作用，男孩便跑向那个身影，一把将他推下了楼梯，他滚了十层台阶，跌在了一个角落里。男孩敲完钟便回屋了，一句话没说就上床睡着了。主持的妻子等了好久都没等来丈夫。终于，她感到不安了，便叫醒男孩，问他：“你知道我丈夫在哪儿吗？他在你之前就到钟楼上去了。”男孩答道：“不，我不知道啊，不过回音洞对面的楼梯上倒是站了一个人，可他既不回答也不走开，我想他是个无赖，就把他推到了楼下，快去看看他是不是你丈夫吧。如果是他，我真的很抱歉啊。”女人跑过去，发现丈夫正躺在角落里呻吟，他摔断了一条腿。 The sexton, however, remained standing motionless that the boy might think he was a ghost.The boy cried a second time, “What do you want here? Speak if thou art an honest fellow, or I will throw thee down the steps!”The sexton thought, “he can’t intend to be as bad as his words,” uttered no sound and stood as if he were made of stone.Then the boy called to him for the third time, and as that was also to no purpose, he ran against him and pushed the ghost down the stairs, so that it fell down ten steps and remained lying there in a corner.Thereupon he rang the bell, went home, and without saying a word went to bed, and fell asleep.The sexton’s wife waited a long time for her husband, but he did not come back.At length she became uneasy, and wakened the boy, and asked, “Dost thou not know where my husband is?He climbed up the tower before thou didst.”“No, I don’t know,” replied the boy, “but some one was standing by the sounding hole on the other side of the steps, and as he would neither give an answer nor go away, I took him for a scoundrel, and threw him downstairs, just go there and you will see if it was he.I should be sorry if it were.”The woman ran away and found her husband, who was lying moaning in the corner, and had broken his leg.
女人将丈夫背回家，接着大喊大叫地跑到了男孩的父亲家中。她喊道：“你儿子真是个祸害啊！他把我丈夫推下楼梯，让他摔断了一条腿。快把这个废物带走吧。”父亲吓坏了，赶紧跑去训斥了儿子一顿。他说：“你干了些什么坏事啊？肯定有恶魔教唆你这么做的。”男孩答道：“爸爸，请听我解释。我是很无辜的。他半夜站在那里，就像个要干坏事的家伙。我不知道他是谁，我要求了他三次，让他要不就说话，要不就走开。”父亲说：“唉，你对我而言只是徒增烦恼。。别让我再看到你。我不想见你。” She carried him down, and then with loud screams she hastened to the boy’s father.“Your boy,” cried she, “has been the cause of a great misfortune!He has thrown my husband down the steps and made him break his leg.Take the good-for-nothing fellow away from our house.”The father was terrified, and ran thither and scolded the boy.“What wicked tricks are these?” said he, “the devil must have put this into thy head.”“Father,” he replied, “do listen to me.I am quite innocent.He was standing there by night like one who is intending to do some evil.I did not know who it was, and I entreated him three times either to speak or to go away.”“Ah,” said the father, “I have nothing but unhappiness with you.Go out of my sight.I will see thee no more.”
“好的，爸爸，我愿意离开，只要等到天亮就行了。我去学习怎样发抖，那样的话，我至少能懂一门手艺，可以养活自己。”父亲说道：“你爱学什么就学什么去吧，对我来说无所谓。给你五十元钱。带上这些钱去闯荡世界吧，别告诉别人你的家乡在哪里，也别告诉别人你的父亲是谁，因为我可不想跟着你丢人。”“好的，爸爸，我会按你说的做。如果你只有这些要求的话，我很容易就能记住。” “Yes, father, right willingly, wait only until it is day.Then will I go forth and learn how to shudder, and then I shall, at any rate, understand one art which will support me.”“Learn what thou wilt,” spake the father, “it is all the same to me.Here are fifty thalers for thee.Take these and go into the wide world, and tell no one from whence thou comest, and who is thy father, for I have reason to be ashamed of thee.”“Yes, father, it shall be as you will.If you desire nothing more than that, I can easily keep it in mind.”
因此，天亮后，男孩将五十元钱塞进口袋里就踏上大路了，他不停地自言自语：“要是我会发抖该多好啊！要是我会发抖该多好啊！”这时，一个人经过，他听到了男孩的话。等他们又走远一点儿到能看到绞刑架的时候，那人对男孩说：“你看，那边大树下的七个人娶了绳匠的女儿，现在正在学习怎样飞翔呢。你到树下坐着，等到夜里你很快就能学会怎样发抖了。”男孩答道：“如果只是这样的话，很容易做到啊。要是我能那么快就学会发抖，那就给你这五十块钱。明天早上你到这儿来找我吧。”于是，男孩坐到绞刑架下面，一直等到夜幕降临。因为觉得冷，男孩便生起一堆火来，可半夜时刮起了大风，尽管有火，他还是感到冷。大风吹得绞刑架上的人互相碰撞、晃来晃去，他心想：“我在下面生着火还冻得发抖呢，他们在上面的一定冻坏了！”他很同情他们，便支起梯子爬了上去，一个一个地把他们七个人都解开扛了下来。然后，他捅捅火，把火吹得旺旺的，把他们排在火周围取暖。可他们纹丝不动地坐在那里，连衣服都烧着了。于是他说道：“当心点，不然我就把你们再挂上去。”可死尸都听不到，仍旧一声不响地任由破衣服燃烧。男孩为此生起气来，说道：“你们还不当心的话，我也帮不了你们了，我可不想跟你们一起被烧死。”他便一个接一个地又将他们挂了上去。然后他坐在火旁睡着了，第二天早上，那人来找他，想拿走那五十元钱，他说：“嘿，这下你知道什么叫发抖了吧？”男孩回答说：“我不知道啊，我怎么能知道呢？树上的那些家伙根本不开口，还呆头呆脑的，身上的衣服烧着了都不管。”那人知道自己是拿不到那五十元钱了，边走边说：“我以前还真是没见过这种人。” When day dawned, therefore, the boy put his fifty thalers into his pocket, and went forth on the great highway, and continually said to himself, “If I could but shudder!If I could but shudder!”Then a man approached who heard this conversation which the youth was holding with himself, and when they had walked a little farther to where they could see the gallows, the man said to him, “Look, there is the tree where seven men have married the ropemaker’s daughter, and are now learning how to fly.Sit down below it, and wait till night comes, and you will soon learn how to shudder.”“If that is all that is wanted,” answered the youth, “it is easily done; but if I learn how to shudder as fast as that, thou shalt have my fifty thalers.Just come back to me early in the morning.”Then the youth went to the gallows, sat down below it, and waited till evening came.And as he was cold, he lighted himself a fire, but at midnight the wind blew so sharply that in spite of his fire, he could not get warm.And as the wind knocked the hanged men against each other, and they moved backwards and forwards, he thought to himself “Thou shiverest below by the fire, but how those up above must freeze and suffer!”And as he felt pity for them, he raised the ladder, and climbed up, unbound one of them after the other, and brought down all seven.Then he stirred the fire, blew it, and set them all round it to warm themselves.But they sat there and did not stir, and the fire caught their clothes.So he said, “Take care, or I will hang you up again.”The dead men, however, did not hear, but were quite silent, and let their rags go on burning.On this he grew angry, and said, “If you will not take care, I cannot help you, I will not be burnt with you,” and he hung them up again each in his turn.Then he sat down by his fire and fell asleep, and the next morning the man came to him and wanted to have the fifty thalers, and said, “Well, dost thou know how to shudder?”“No,” answered he, “how was I to get to know?Those fellows up there did not open their mouths, and were so stupid that they let the few old rags which they had on their bodies get burnt.”Then the man saw that he would not get the fifty thalers that day, and went away saying, “One of this kind has never come my way before.”
男孩也继续赶路，又自言自语地说：“唉，我要是会发抖该多好啊！唉，我要是会发抖该多好啊！”他身后的一位车夫听到男孩的话后，问道：“你是谁？”男孩答道：“我不知道。”于是车夫又问：“你从哪里来呢？”“我不知道。”“你父亲叫什么名字呢？”“我不能告诉你。”“你一直在嘟嚷些什么呢？”男孩回答说：“唉，我真希望自己会发抖啊，可没人能教会我。”车夫说：“别再说这些傻话了。来，跟我走吧，我领你去个地方。”男孩跟着车夫走了，晚上的时候他们来到了一家旅店，准备在那里借宿一夜。到了房间门口，男孩又大声地说：“我要是会发抖该多好啊！我要是会发抖该多好啊！”店主听到后大笑起来，说：“如果那是你的心愿，这里倒是有个机会给你呢。”老板娘说：“嘿，安静点，那么多好奇的人都送了命，要是他这样一双漂亮的眼睛永远合上了，那多可惜、多遗憾啊。” The youth likewise went his way, and once more began to mutter to himself, “Ah, if I could but shudder!Ah, if I could but shudder!”A waggoner who was striding behind him heard that and asked, “Who are you?”“I don’t know,” answered the youth.Then the waggoner asked, “From whence comest thou?”“I know not.”“Who is thy father?”“That I may not tell thee.”“What is it that thou art always muttering between thy teeth.”“Ah,” replied the youth, “I do so wish I could shudder, but no one can teach me how to do it.”“Give up thy foolish chatter,” said the waggoner.“Come, go with me, I will see about a place for thee.”The youth went with the waggoner, and in the evening they arrived at an inn where they wished to pass the night.Then at the entrance of the room the youth again said quite loudly, “If I could but shudder!If I could but shudder!”The host who heard this, laughed and said, “If that is your desire, there ought to be a good opportunity for you here.”“Ah, be silent,” said the hostess, “so many inquisitive persons have already lost their lives, it would be a pity and a shame if such beautiful eyes as these should never see the daylight again.”
可男孩却说：“不管有多艰难我都要学会发抖，我就是为了这个目标才离开家的。”他不停地缠着店主，店主只得告诉他说，离这里不远处有一座闹鬼的城堡，任何人只要去那里看守三夜，保证会学会发抖。国王曾承诺将自己的女儿嫁给敢于去冒险的人，她可是世界上最漂亮的人啊。城堡里还有很多金银财宝，可是都由魔鬼看守着。这些财宝足够让一个穷人变成富翁了。已经有很多人都前去冒险了，可到现在还没人能活着走出来。于是，男孩第二天一早就去觐见国王，他请求国王允许他去城堡里看守三夜。国王打量了一下这个年轻人，因为这个年轻人让他满意，国王便说：“你可以随身带上三件东西，不过不能带活物。”男孩答道：“那我就带一个火种，一台车床和一个配有刀的菜板。”白天，国王命人将这三件东西送进了城堡里。夜幕快要降临的时候，男孩走进了城堡，他在一间屋子里生起了旺旺的火，火旁放了配有刀的菜板，然后坐到了车床旁。他说：“唉，我要是会发抖该多好啊！可我在这里也不可能学会。”半夜时分，他准备捅捅火，正用力吹的时候，一个角落里忽然传来一声喊叫：“啊，喵！真是冻死了！” But the youth said, “However difficult it may be, I will learn it and for this purpose indeed have I journeyed forth.”He let the host have no rest, until the latter told him, that not far from thence stood a haunted castle where any one could very easily learn what shuddering was, if he would but watch in it for three nights.The King had promised that he who would venture should have his daughter to wife, and she was the most beautiful maiden the sun shone on.Great treasures likewise lay in the castle, which were guarded by evil spirits, and these treasures would then be freed, and would make a poor man rich enough.Already many men had gone into the castle, but as yet none had come out again.Then the youth went next morning to the King and said if he were allowed he would watch three nights in the haunted castle.The King looked at him, and as the youth pleased him, he said, “Thou mayest ask for three things to take into the castle with thee, but they must be things without life.”Then he answered, “Then I ask for a fire, a turning lathe, and a cutting board with the knife.”The King had these things carried into the castle for him during the day.When night was drawing near, the youth went up and made himself a bright fire in one of the rooms, placed the cutting board and knife beside it, and seated himself by the turning lathe.“Ah, if I could but shudder!” said he, “but I shall not learn it here either.”Towards midnight he was about to poke his fire, and as he was blowing it, something cried suddenly from one corner, “Au, miau! How cold we are!”
男孩喊道：“你们这些笨蛋！在吵什么啊？你们要是冷的话，就过来坐在火旁取暖啊。”他刚说完，两只大黑猫就猛地一跳蹿了出来，分别坐到了他的两边，怒目圆瞪，狠狠地盯着他。一会儿后，它们都暖和过来了，便说：“朋友，我们一起玩牌吧。”男孩答道：“为什么不呢？不过先让我看看你们的爪子。”于是黑猫伸出爪子来。男孩说：“啊，你们的指甲太长了！等等，我得先把你们的长指甲剪剪。”于是，男孩揪住黑猫的脖子，一把将它们摁到菜板上，死死地捆住了它们的爪子。他说：“我一看到你们的爪子，就完全没了和你们玩牌的兴致。”他打死两只猫后，将它们扔到了外面的水池里。他除掉了两只猫，正要靠着火堆坐下的时候，忽然从四面八方冒出来很多黑猫和黑狗，它们的脖子上都套着烧得通红的铁链。猫狗越来越多，最后他已被挤得动弹不得，它们发出恐怖的叫声，跳到火上搅弄着，想把火熄灭。他静静地看了它们一会儿，最后它们折腾得太过分了，他终于无法忍受，便抓起菜刀喝道：“你们这群混蛋，都滚远点。”说完就挥舞着菜刀朝它们砍去。一些猫狗逃走了，一些被他砍死后丢到了鱼池里。回到屋里后，他把火吹旺以取暖。他坐着坐着，就觉得上下眼皮开始打架了，很想睡觉。他四处望了一下，看到角落里有一张大床。他说：“我正需要一张床呢。”于是躺了上去。可是，他正要闭上眼睛，床竟然自己动起来，在城堡里转来转去。他说：“太好了，再走快一点。”于是，床就像被六匹马拉着似的飞快地来回走着，它跨过门槛和楼梯，忽然跳了起来，一下子就翻了个身，像一座大山似的将男孩压在了下面。然而，他先把被子和枕头扔出来，然后自己也钻了出来，说道：“现在谁爱到床上睡就去睡吧。”接着躺到火旁，一觉睡到了天亮。早上国王来到城堡，看到男孩躺在地上，还以为他已被魔鬼杀死了。于是国王感叹道：“真是可惜啊，这么英俊的一个孩子。”男孩听到后便起身了，说道：“还没到那一步呢。”国王又惊又喜，他问男孩夜里发生的是怎样的情况。男孩答道：“还不错，一夜已经过去了，剩下的两夜也会一样过去的。”然后，他回到了旅店老板那里，店主瞪大眼睛说：“我没想到还能再见到活着的你啊！你学会怎样发抖了吗？”男孩说：“还没有，都是徒劳啊。要是有个人能教会我该多好啊。” “You simpletons!” cried he, “what are you crying about?If you are cold, come and take a seat by the fire and warm yourselves.”And when he had said that, two great black cats came with one tremendous leap and sat down on each side of him, and looked savagely at him with their fiery eyes.After a short time, when they had warmed themselves, they said, “Comrade, shall we have a game at cards?”“Why not?” he replied, “but just show me your paws.”Then they stretched out their claws.“Oh,” said he, “what long nails you have!Wait, I must first cut them for you.”Thereupon he seized them by the throats, put them on the cutting board and screwed their feet fast.“I have looked at your fingers,” said he, “and my fancy for card playing has gone,” and he struck them dead and threw them out into the water.But when he had made away with these two, and was about to sit down again by his fire, out from every hole and corner came black cats and black dogs with red-hot chains, and more and more of them came until he could no longer stir, and they yelled horribly, and got on his fire, pulled it to pieces, and tried to put it out.He watched them for a while quietly, but at last when they were going too far, he seized his cutting knife, and cried, “Away with ye, vermin,” and began to cut them down.Part of them ran away, the others he killed, and threw out into the fish pond.When he came back he fanned the embers of his fire again and warmed himself.And as he thus sat, his eyes would keep open no longer, and he felt a desire to sleep.Then he looked round and saw a great bed in the corner.“That is the very thing for me,” said he, and got into it.When he was just going to shut his eyes, however, the bed began to move of its own accord, and went over the whole of the castle.“That’s right,” said he, “but go faster.”Then the bed rolled on as if six horses were harnessed to it, up and down, over thresholds and steps, but suddenly hop, hop, it turned over upside down, and lay on him like a mountain.But he threw quilts and pillows up in the air, got out and said, “Now any one who likes, may drive,” and lay down by his fire, and slept till it was day.In the morning the King came, and when he saw him lying there on the ground, he thought the evil spirits had killed him and he was dead.Then said he, “After all it is a pity, he is a handsome man.”The youth heard it, got up, and said, “It has not come to that yet.”Then the King was astonished, but very glad, and asked how he had fared.“Very well indeed,” answered he; “one night is past, the two others will get over likewise.”Then he went to the innkeeper, who opened his eyes very wide, and said, “I never expected to see thee alive again!Hast thou learnt how to shudder yet?”“No,” said he, “it is all in vain.If some one would but tell me.”
第二天晚上，男孩又来到城堡里，他坐在火旁，又嘟嚷起来：“要是我会发抖该多好啊。”午夜时分，他听到了吵吵嚷嚷翻滚的声音。开始时声音很小，可是变得越来越大。又安静了片刻后，忽然传来一声尖叫，一个只有半个身子的人从烟囱掉到了男孩面前。男孩喊道：“你好啊！你还少一半身子呢。这样可不行！”又传来一阵吵嚷声，还有怒吼和哀号的声音，另一半身子也从烟囱里掉了下来。男孩说：“等着，我给你把火烧旺点。”他把火烧旺后，四周一看才发现，那两半身体已经接在了一起，变为一个面目狰狞的人，正坐在男孩的位置上。男孩说：“我们可没商量过啊，这凳子是我的。”那人想把男孩推走，而男孩坚持不让，用尽力气把他推下凳子，然后坐回自己的位置。后来，越来越多的人一个接一个地掉下来，他们拿出九条死人腿和两个死人脑袋，玩起了九柱戏。男孩也想玩，便说：“我说，我能加入你们吗？”“可以啊，只要你有钱就行。”男孩答道：“我有的是钱，可你们的球不太圆啊。”于是，他拿起死人头，放到车床上车得圆圆的。他说：“这样它就更容易滚起来了。”“好哇！它滚得真顺溜啊！” The second night he again went up into the old castle, sat down by the fire, and once more began his old song, “If I could but shudder.”When midnight came, an uproar and noise of tumbling about was heard; at first it was low, but it grew louder and louder.Then it was quiet for awhile, and at length with a loud scream, half a man came down the chimney and fell before him.“Hollo!” cried he, “another half belongs to this.This is too little!”Then the uproar began again, there was a roaring and howling, and the other half fell down likewise.“Wait,” said he, “I will just blow up the fire a little for thee.”When he had done that and looked round again, the two pieces were joined together, and a frightful man was sitting in his place.“That is no part of our bargain,” said the youth, “the bench is mine.”The man wanted to push him away; the youth, however, would not allow that, but thrust him off with all his strength, and seated himself again in his own place.Then still more men fell down, one after the other; they brought nine dead men’s legs and two skulls, and set them up and played at ninepins with them.The youth also wanted to play and said “Hark you, can I join you?”“Yes, if thou hast any money.”“Money enough,” replied he, “but your balls are not quite round.”Then he took the skulls and put them in the lathe and turned them till they were round.“There, now, they will roll better!” said he.“Hurrah! Now it goes merrily!”
男孩和他们一起玩游戏，输了一些钱。可当钟敲响十二点的时候，一切都消失了。他便躺下，踏踏实实地睡了一觉。第二天早上，国王来询问男孩情况。他问道：“这次你是怎么度过的呢？”男孩回答说：“我们玩了一会儿九柱戏，输了一些钱。”“你还是没发抖吗？”男孩说：“啊，什么啊？我玩得高兴着呢。要是我会发抖就好了！” He played with them and lost some of his money, but when it struck twelve, everything vanished from his sight.He lay down and quietly fell asleep.Next morning the King came to inquire after him.“How has it fared with you this time?” asked he.“I have been playing at ninepins,” he answered, “and have lost a couple of farthings.”“Hast thou not shuddered then?”“Eh, what?” said he, “I have made merry.If I did but know what it was to shudder!”
第三天夜里，他又坐在凳子上，伤心地说：“我要是会发抖该多好啊。”到深夜的时候，来了六个大块头的人，他们还带来一副棺材。男孩说：“啊哈，这肯定是我几天前刚去世的表弟。”他挥挥手，喊道：“过来，我的小表弟，快过来。”他们把棺材放到地上，男孩跑过去，打开棺材盖，里面躺着一个死人。他摸了摸那人的脸，真是冰冷冰冷的。他说道：“等一下，我给你暖一暖。”男孩走到火旁，把手烤热，然后再放到死人的脸上，可他还是冰冷的。于是，男孩把尸体抱出来坐在火旁，他让尸体躺在自己怀里，不停地给尸体按摩胳膊，想让血液再循环起来。可这样还是无济于事，他心想：“两个人一起躺到床上的话，就能互相取暖啊。”于是，他将尸体抱上床，盖上被子，然后在尸体身边躺了下来。不一会儿后，尸体也暖和过来了，开始活动起来。男孩便说：“你看，小表弟，我这不是把你暖和过来了吗？”可是，死人却爬起来，喊道：“现在我要勒死你。” The third night he sat down again on his bench and said quite sadly, “If I could but shudder.”When it grew late, six tall men came in and brought a coffin.Then said he, “Ha, ha, that is certainly my little cousin, who died only a few days ago,” and he beckoned with his finger, and cried “Come, little cousin, come.”They placed the coffin on the ground, but he went to it and took the lid off, and a dead man lay therein.He felt his face, but it was cold as ice.“Stop,” said he, “I will warm thee a little,” and went to the fire and warmed his hand and laid it on the dead man’s face, but he remained cold.Then he took him out, and sat down by the fire and laid him on his breast and rubbed his arms that the blood might circulate again.As this also did no good, he thought to himself “When two people lie in bed together, they warm each other,” and carried him to the bed, covered him over and lay down by him.After a short time the dead man became warm too, and began to move.Then said the youth, “See, little cousin, have I not warmed thee?”The dead man, however, got up and cried, “Now will I strangle thee.”
男孩说：“什么？！你就准备这样来感谢我？你真该立即滚回棺材里去。”男孩抱起尸体，一把扔进了棺材里，然后合上了盖子。然后，那六个大块头又过来把棺材搬走了。他说：“我还是不会发抖。我可能在这里永远都学不会发抖。” “What!” said he, “is that the way thou thankest me?Thou shalt at once go into thy coffin again,” and he took him up, threw him into it, and shut the lid.Then came the six men and carried him away again.“I cannot manage to shudder,” said he.“I shall never learn it here as long as I live.”
这时，走进来一个人，比其他人都要高，他的样子很可怕。可是，他已经很老了，还长着一把长长的白胡子。他喊道：“你这个小矮子，你很快就知道什么是发抖了，因为我就要杀了你。”男孩答道：“还没那么快。如果我要死，也得先说几句话。”恶魔说：“我要马上抓住你。”“别急，别急啊，不要说大话。我和你一样强壮，甚至比你还要强壮呢。”老恶魔说：“我们走着瞧吧。如果你更强壮，我就放你走。来，我们比试比试。”于是，他带着男孩穿过一条漆黑的过道，来到了一个炼钢厂。他拿起一把斧子，一下子将铁砧砸进了地里。男孩说：“我可比你厉害。”说完走向另一个铁砧。老恶魔站得近些，想要亲眼看一看，他的白胡子朝下垂着。男孩抓起斧子，只一下就将铁砧劈成了两半，而老恶魔的胡子也被夹了进去。男孩说道：“现在我抓到你了。现在是你要送命了。”男孩抓起一根铁棍，狠狠地朝老恶魔打去，直打得他嗷嗷乱叫，恳请男孩别打了，还承诺要给男孩很多财宝。男孩便抽出斧子，饶了他一命。老恶魔带着男孩回到城堡，给他看了地下室的三大箱金子。恶魔说：“这些金子送给穷人一箱，送给国王一箱，剩下的一箱就是给你的了。”这时，钟声敲响了十二点，恶魔忽然消失了。于是，男孩被独自留在了黑暗的地窖里。他说道：“我还是能找到出去的路。”他在四周摸索着，终于找到了通向房间的道路，又在那里靠着火堆睡了一夜。第二天早上，国王来了，说：“现在你一定学会发抖了吧？”男孩答道：“没有，发抖到底是什么啊？我死去的表弟来过这儿，还有一个胡子老头给我看了地窖里的很多钱，可没人告诉我什么是发抖。”国王说：“这么说你可是拯救了这座城堡啊，你可以娶我的女儿为妻。”男孩说道：“那可太好了，可我还是不知道什么叫发抖啊。” Then a man entered who was taller than all others, and looked terrible.He was old, however, and had a long white beard.“Thou wretch,” cried he, “thou shalt soon learn what it is to shudder, for thou shalt die.”“Not so fast,” replied the youth.“If I am to die, I shall have to have a say in it.”“I will soon seize thee,” said the fiend.“Softly, softly, do not talk so big.I am as strong as thou art, and perhaps even stronger.”“We shall see,” said the old man.“If thou art stronger, I will let thee go-come, we will try.”Then he led him by dark passages to a smith’s forge, took an axe, and with one blow struck an anvil into the ground.“I can do better than that,” said the youth, and went to the other anvil.The old man placed himself near and wanted to look on, and his white beard hung down.Then the youth seized the axe, split the anvil with one blow, and struck the old man’s beard in with it.“Now I have thee,” said the youth.“Now it is thou who will have to die.”Then he seized an iron bar and beat the old man till he moaned and entreated him to stop, and he would give him great riches.The youth drew out the axe and let him go.The old man led him back into the castle, and in a cellar showed him three chests full of gold.“Of these,” said he, “one part is for the poor, the other for the king, the third is thine.”In the meantime it struck twelve, and the spirit disappeared; the youth, therefore, was left in darkness.“I shall still be able to find my way out,” said he, and felt about, found the way into the room, and slept there by his fire.Next morning the King came and said “Now thou must have learnt what shuddering is?”“No,” he answered; “what can it be?My dead cousin was here, and a bearded man came and showed me a great deal of money down below, but no one told me what it was to shudder.”“Then,” said the King, “thou hast delivered the castle, and shalt marry my daughter.”“That is all very well,” said he, “but still I do not know what it is to shudder.”
金子被搬了上来，男孩和公主也举行了婚礼。可是，尽管他很爱自己的妻子，也生活得很开心，他还总是说“要是我会发抖该多好啊。”最后，公主忍无可忍，生起气来。她的女佣说：“我有个好办法，他很快就知道什么是发抖了。”她来到流经花园的那条小河边，捞了满满一桶鮈鱼带给公主。晚上，年轻的国王酣睡时，公主悄悄将他的衣服脱下来，然后把满满一桶盛着鮈鱼的凉水都倒到了男孩的身上，鮈鱼在他的身上蹿来蹿去。这时，男孩一下子醒过来，喊道：“啊，亲爱的妻子，是什么让我颤抖起来？是什么让我颤抖起来？啊！我终于学会发抖了！” Then the gold was brought up and the wedding celebrated; but howsoever much the young king loved his wife, and however happy he was, he still said always “If I could but shudder,—if I could but shudder.”And at last she was angry at this.Her waiting maid said, “I will find a cure for him; he shall soon learn what it is to shudder.”She went out to the stream which flowed through the garden, and had a whole bucketful of gudgeons brought to her.At night when the young king was sleeping, his wife was to draw the clothes off him and empty the bucketful of cold water with the gudgeons in it over him, so that the little fishes would sprawl about him.When this was done, he woke up and cried “Oh, what makes me shudder so? —what makes me shudder so, dear wife?Ah! Now I know what it is to shudder！”
三只小鸟 The Three Little Birds
大约一千多年以前，这个国家里生活着许多小国国王，其中有一位住在科哀特山上的国王非常喜欢打猎。一次，国王带着他的猎手们骑着马出宫去打猎，途中有三个女孩正在山上放牛，女孩们看到国王和他的随从后，那个年龄最大的女孩便指着国王，朝另外两个女孩说道：“我非他不嫁。”另一个女孩指着国王右手边的人，从山的另一边喊道：“嘿！嘿！我非他不嫁。” About a thousand or more years ago, there were in this country nothing but small kings, and one of them who lived on the Keuterberg was very fond of hunting.Once on a time when he was riding forth from his castle with his huntsmen, three girls were watching their cows upon the mountain, and when they saw the King with all his followers, the eldest girl pointed to him, and called to the two other girls, “If I do not get that one, I will have none.”Then the second girl answered from the other side of the hill, and pointed to the one who was on the King’s right hand, “Hilloa! If I do not get him, I will have no one.”
国王左右手边的两个人正是他的两个大臣。国王听到了女孩们的全部对话，他狩猎回来时，便命人将这三个女孩带到面前，问她们昨天在山上说了什么。女孩们都不言语，于是国王便问那个年龄最大的女孩是不是真的想嫁给他。女孩便只好说：“是的。”两位大臣也娶了另两个女孩，因为她们三人都很漂亮，尤其是王后，她长着一头亚麻色的长发。可是，年龄较小的那两个女孩一直都没能生孩子。一次，国王需要出趟宫，便请她俩来陪王后，让她开心，因为王后就要生小孩了。王后产下了一个小王子，小王子出生时身上就长有一颗颜色鲜艳的红痣。王后的两个妹妹商量着要将漂亮的小王子扔到河里。当她们将小王子扔下去时——我猜是扔到了威悉河吧——一只小鸟飞向了天空，唱道：“你是否能活下来呀，只有上帝才知道。变成一朵百合花吧，勇敢的宝贝，那就是你的居所。” These, however, were the two ministers.The King heard all this, and when he had come back from the chase, he caused the three girls to be brought to him, and asked them what they had said yesterday on the mountain.This they would not tell him, so the King asked the eldest if she really would take him for her husband?Then she said, “Yes,” and the two ministers married the two sisters, for they were all three fair and beautiful of face, especially the Queen, who had hair like flax.But the two sisters had no children, and once when the King was obliged to go from home he invited them to come to the Queen in order to cheer her, for she was about to bear a child.She had a little boy who brought a bright red star into the world with him.Then the two sisters said to each other that they would throw the beautiful boy into the water.When they had thrown him in (I believe it was into the Weser) a little bird flew up into the air, which sang ,“To thy death art thou sped, Until God’s word be said.In the white lily bloom, Brave boy, is thy tomb.”
两个女人听后，害怕极了，便匆匆跑走了。国王回来以后，她们告诉国王说，王后产下了一条小狗。国王说：“上帝做的肯定是对的！”然而，住在河边的一个渔夫救起了小王子，他还活着。渔夫夫妇没有子女，便收养了他。一年过去后，国王又出远门了，王后生下另一个小王子，可那两个虚伪的妹妹又将他扔到了河里。一只小鸟飞向天空，唱道：“你能否活下来呀，还不知道呢。变成一朵百合花吧，勇敢的宝贝，那就是你的居所。” When the two heard that, they were frightened to death, and ran away in great haste.When the King came home they told him that the Queen had been delivered of a dog.Then the King said, “What God does, is well done!”But a fisherman who dwelt near the water fished the little boy out again while he was still alive, and as his wife had no children, they reared him.When a year had gone by, the King again went away, and the Queen had another little boy, whom the false sisters likewise took and threw into the water.Then up flew a little bird again and sang,“To thy death art thou sped, Until God’s word be said.In the white lily bloom, Brave boy, is thy tomb.”
国王回来后，两个女人告诉他说，王后又生下了一只小狗，国王又说道：“上帝做的肯定是对的。”然而，渔夫又救了这位小王子一命，收养了他。 And when the King came back, they told him that the Queen had once more given birth to a dog, and he again said, “What God does, is well done.”The fisherman, however, fished this one also out of the water, and reared him.
国王第三次出远门了，这次王后产下了一个小公主，可她那两个虚伪的妹妹还是将小公主丢进了河里。小鸟又一次飞向天空，唱道：“你能否活下来呀，还不知道呢。变成一朵百合花吧，漂亮的宝贝，那就是你的居所。” Then the King again journeyed forth, and the Queen had a little girl, whom also the false sisters threw into the water.Then again a little bird flew up on high and sang,“To thy death art thou sped Until God’s word be said.In the white lily bloom, Bonny girl, is thy tomb.”
国王回来后，两个女人告诉国王说，王后生下了一只小猫。于是国王发怒了，他命人将王后锁进了大牢里，王后在那里一关就是好些年。 And when the King came home they told him that the Queen had been delivered of a cat.Then the King grew angry, and ordered his wife to be cast into prison, and therein was she shut up for many long years.
这段时间里，孩子们也都长大了。一次，年龄最大的男孩想要同其他孩子一起去打渔，可是他们却不愿与他结伴，都说：“走开，你这个没人要的家伙。” In the meantime the children had grown up.Then eldest once went out with some other boys to fish, but the other boys would not have him with them, and said, “Go thy way, foundling.”
他为此非常烦恼，便问渔夫这是不是真的。渔夫告诉他，一次去打渔时从水里救出了他。于是男孩说，他要去寻找自己的亲生父亲。渔夫恳求他留下来，可男孩心意已决，最后渔夫只得同意了。男孩就这样上路了，他走了很多天，最后来到一条大河前，河边站着一位钓鱼的老婆婆。男孩说：“你好，老婆婆。” Hereupon he was much troubled, and asked the old fisherman if that was true.The fisherman told him that once when he was fishing he had drawn him out of the water.So the boy said he would go forth and seek his father.The fisherman, however, entreated him to stay, but he would not let himself be hindered, and at last the fisherman consented.Then the boy went on his way and walked for many days, and at last he came to a great piece of water by the side of which stood an old woman fishing.“Good day, mother,” said the boy.
老婆婆回道：“谢谢你。” “Many thanks,” said she.
“你要很长时间才能钓到一条鱼吧？” “Thou wilt fish long enough before thou catchest anything.”
“你也要花很长时间才能找到你的父亲的。你怎么过河呢？”老婆婆问。 “And thou wilt seek long enough before thou findest thy father.How wilt thou get over the water?” said the woman.
“天知道。” “God knows.”
于是，老婆婆背起他过了河。他寻找了很长时间，可还是没有找到父亲。 Then the old woman took him up on her back and carried him through it, and he sought for a long time, but could not find his father.
一年过去了，另一个男孩动身去寻找他的哥哥。他来到河边，和他哥哥经历的一样。现在，渔夫家里只剩下女儿了，她非常想念两个哥哥，最终也说服渔夫让她前去寻找他们了。于是，女孩也来到了大河边，她对老婆婆说：“你好，老婆婆。” When a year had gone by, the second boy set out to seek his brother.He came to the water, and all fared with him just as with his brother.And now there was no one at home but the daughter, and she mourned for her brothers so much that at last she also begged the fisherman to let her set forth, for she wished to go in search of her brothers.Then she likewise came to the great piece of water, and she said to the old woman, “Good day, mother.”
老婆婆答道：“谢谢你。” “Many thanks,” replied the old woman.
女孩说：“愿上帝保佑你钓到大鱼。”听闻此言，老婆婆变得非常和蔼，她把女孩带过河去，还送给她一根魔杖，她对女孩说：“去吧，我的孩子，顺着这条大路走，遇到一条大黑狗时，你要悄悄地勇敢从它身边走过去，既不能笑也不能看它。然后你就会到达一座宏伟的宫殿，你站上门槛的时候，要立即扔掉魔杖，径直穿过宫殿，从另一边出来。出来后你会看到一口古井，井里长出一棵大树，大树上挂着一个关了一只小鸟的笼子，你要把笼子取下来。再从井里舀一杯水，带着这两样东西沿原路返回。走到门槛时，再把那根魔杖捡起来带走；再碰到那条狗后就用魔杖打它的脸，一定要确定打到了它，然后回到我这里来。”女孩发现一切真如老婆婆所说的那样，在回来的路上，她还找到了她那两个走遍半个世界彼此寻找的哥哥。他们一起来到那个地方，那条黑狗正躺在路上，女孩用魔杖打它的脸，黑狗竟变成了一位英俊的王子，王子和他们一起来到河边。老婆婆还站在那里。她很高兴又见到了他们，带他们一起渡过大河，然后就走开了，因为她身上的魔法也被解除了。四个年轻人一起回到了老渔夫的家，大家都因再次重逢而兴奋不已，还把鸟笼子挂在了墙壁上。 “May God help you with your fishing,” said the maiden.When the old woman heard that, she became quite friendly, and carried her over the water, gave her a wand, and said to her, “Go, my daughter, ever onwards by this road, and when you come to a great black dog, you must pass it silently and boldly, without either laughing or looking at it.Then you will come to a great high castle, on the threshold of which you must let the wand fall, and go straight through the castle, and out again on the other side.There you will see an old fountain out of which a large tree has grown, whereon hangs a bird in a cage which you must take down.Take likewise a glass of water out of the fountain, and with these two things go back by the same way.Pick up the wand again from the threshold and take it with you, and when you again pass by the dog, strike him in the face with it, but be sure that you hit him, and then just come back here to me.”The maiden found everything exactly as the old woman had said, and on her way back she found her two brothers who had sought each other over half the world.They went together to the place where the black dog was lying on the road; she struck it in the face, and it turned into a handsome prince who went with them to the river.There the old woman was still standing.She rejoiced much to see them again, and carried them all over the water, and then she too went away, for now she was freed.The others, however, went to the old fisherman, and all were glad that they had found each other again, but they hung the bird on the wall.
可是，二儿子总是没办法憋在家里，他拿上弓箭就去打猎了。他感觉累了，便拿出长笛，吹起了曲子。国王当时也在打猎，他顺着笛声走过来，看到男孩后，便说道：“谁让你在这里打猎的？” But the second son could not settle at home, and took his cross-bow and went a hunting.When he was tired he took his flute, and made music.The King was hunting too, and heard that and went thither, and when he met the youth, he said, “Who has given thee leave to hunt here?”
“噢，没有人。” “Oh, no one.”
“你是谁家的孩子呢？” “To whom dost thou belong, then?”
“我是渔夫的儿子。” “I am the fisherman’s son.”
“可他没有子女啊。” “But he has no children.”
“你要是不相信的话，就跟我一起来吧。” “If thou wilt not believe, come with me.”
于是，国王随男孩回了家，他问渔夫是怎么回事，渔夫便将一切如实告诉了国王，墙上的小鸟开始唱起歌来： That the King did, and questioned the fisherman, who told everything to him, and the little bird on the wall began to sing,
“他们的母亲被独自锁在大牢里，敬爱的国王啊，他们都是你的孩子。他们的姨妈太狠毒，想要害死孩子们，将他们丢进大河里，幸亏被渔夫救起来。” “The mother sits alone There in the prison small, O King of royal blood, These are thy children all.The sisters twain so false, They wrought the children woe, There in the waters deep Where the fishermen come and go.”
听到这首歌，大家都惊呆了，国王带上这只小鸟、渔夫，还有三个孩子一起回到了王宫里，他命人打开牢门，把妻子放了出来。可是，王后已经生病了，变得非常虚弱。女儿便给她喝了从古井里取出的水，王后马上就恢复健康了。而那两个狠毒的姨妈被烧死了，小公主则嫁给了那位被救的王子。 Then they were all terrified, and the King took the bird, the fisherman and the three children back with him to the castle, and ordered the prison to be opened and brought his wife out again.She had, however, grown quite ill and weak.Then the daughter gave her some of the water of the fountain to drink, and she became strong and healthy.But the two false sisters were burnt, and the daughter married the prince.
胆大的小裁缝 The Valiant Little Tailor
一个夏日的早晨，有一个小裁缝愉快地坐在窗前的桌子旁，全神贯注地缝着衣服。这时，街上传来一个农妇的叫卖声：“卖果酱啦！又好又便宜的果酱啦！卖果酱啦！又好又便宜的果酱啦！”小裁缝听了满心欢喜，小脑袋探出窗外，对农妇喊道：“亲爱的夫人，上来一趟吧，在这儿你会卖掉不少货。”那个农妇扛着她那沉甸甸的篮子爬上了三级台阶，小裁缝让她把所有的果酱都拿出来。他将每瓶果酱都一一举起来，用鼻子闻闻，挑了又挑，最后说：“果酱看起来不错，夫人，给我来四盎司吧，要是四分之一磅也没关系。”本来以为会做成一笔大生意的农妇无可奈何地给他称好果酱，气恼地抱怨着离开了。小裁缝大声喊道：“上帝保佑！您不仅赐给了我健康和力量，还赐给我这么好的果酱。” 他从橱柜里拿出面包，切下一片，然后把果酱涂在上面。“肯定味道不错，”他自言自语道，“但是在吃之前，我得先把这件夹克做好。”他放下面包，继续穿针引线。他满怀喜悦，针脚也越来越大。然而，果酱甜甜的香味弥漫到了整个房间，也飘到了墙上栖息的一大群苍蝇的鼻子里，它们被吸引住了，成群结队地飞到涂着果酱的面包上。“天啊，谁邀请你们这些东西过来的？”小裁缝一边嚷道，一边驱逐着这群不速之客。 One summer’s morning a little tailor was sitting on his table by the window; he was in good spirits, and sewed with all his might.Then came a peasant woman down the street crying, “Good jams, cheap!Good jams, cheap!”This rang pleasantly in the tailor’s ears; he stretched his delicate head out of the window, and called, “Come up here, dear woman; here you will get rid of your goods.”The woman came up the three steps to the tailor with her heavy basket, and he made her unpack the whole of the pots for him.He inspected all of them, lifted them up, put his nose to them, and at length said, “The jam seems to me to be good, so weigh me out four ounces, dear woman, and if it is a quarter of a pound that is of no consequence.”The woman who had hoped to find a good sale, gave him what he desired, but went away quite angry and grumbling.“Now, God bless the jam to my use,” cried the little tailor, “and give me health and strength;” so he brought the bread out of the cupboard, cut himself a piece right across the loaf and spread the jam over it.“This won’t taste bitter,” said he, “but I will just finish the jacket before I take a bite.”He laid the bread near him, sewed on, and in his joy, made bigger and bigger stitches.In the meantime the smell of the sweet jam ascended so to the wall, where the flies were sitting in great numbers, that they were attracted and descended on it in hosts.“Hola! who invited you?” said the little tailor, and drove the unbidden guests away.
然而，这群苍蝇显然不懂德语，无论小裁缝怎么驱赶都无济于事，转眼间就又飞回到面包上去了，而且数目越来越多。小裁缝终于失去了耐心，他从工作台下的柜子里取出一块布，说道：“等着瞧，我让你们吃个够。”　他甩起手中的布，狠狠地朝它们抽去。他放下布，数了数，发现至少有七只苍蝇死挺挺地躺在他面前。 “哼，对付你们还不是小菜一碟。” 他说到，不禁为自己的壮举而感到自豪。“应该让整个镇子都知道我的这个壮举！”小裁缝匆匆给自己剪了一条腰带，把它缝好，然后在上面绣上八个大字：“轻轻一击，七个毙命”。“不行，这个小镇算什么！”他说道，“应当让全世界都知道我的壮举！”他的心快活得无法平静，像小羊羔的尾巴一样大摇大摆。小裁缝系好腰带，下定决心走遍全世界，因为他认为这个小裁缝铺已丝毫容不下像他这样的大英雄了。出发之前，他在屋子里到处搜寻了一番，看是否有能够带走的东西。然而，除了一块放了很久的奶酪，他什么都没有找到。他就把那块奶酪塞到了口袋里。在门外，他看到了一只困在灌木丛中的小鸟。可怜的小鸟也被他塞到了装有奶酪的口袋里。终于，他雄纠纠气昂昂地出发了。他脚步轻快，身手敏捷，丝毫不觉得疲累。他爬上了一座山。当他到达山顶的时候，看到一个身材魁梧的巨人坐在那里，他正悠然自得地上下打量着自己。小裁缝勇敢地迎了上去，对巨人说：“你好啊，先生。你是在这里欣赏大千世界么？我打算走遍天下，去碰碰运气。你有兴趣跟我一起去吗？”巨人轻蔑地看着小裁缝，说道：“哼，穷小子！可怜虫！” The flies, however, who understood no German, would not be turned away, but came back again in ever-increasing companies.The little tailor at last lost all patience, and got a bit of cloth from the hole under his work-table, and saying, “Wait, and I will give it to you,” struck it mercilessly on them.When he drew it away and counted, there lay before him no fewer than seven, dead and with legs stretched out.“Art thou a fellow of that sort?” said he, and could not help admiring his own bravery.“The whole town shall know of this!”And the little tailor hastened to cut himself a girdle, stitched it, and embroidered on it in large letters, “Seven at one stroke!”“What, the town!” he continued, “The whole world shall hear of it!” and his heart wagged with joy like a lamb’s tail.The tailor put on the girdle, and resolved to go forth into the world, because he thought his workshop was too small for his valour.Before he went away, he sought about in the house to see if there was anything which he could take with him; however, he found nothing but an old cheese, and that he put in his pocket.In front of the door he observed a bird which had caught itself in the thicket.It had to go into his pocket with the cheese.Now he took to the road boldly, and as he was light and nimble, he felt no fatigue.The road led him up a mountain, and when he had reached the highest point of it, there sat a powerful giant looking about him quite comfortably.The little tailor went bravely up, spoke to him, and said, “Good day, comrade, so thou art sitting there overlooking the wide-spread world!I am just on my way thither, and want to try my luck.Hast thou any inclination to go with me?”The giant looked contemptuously at the tailor, and said, “Thou ragamuffin! Thou miserable creature!”
“哦，是吗？”小裁缝说道。他解开外套，露出腰带给巨人看，说：“这下你该知道我是什么人了吧！”巨人看到上面绣的“轻轻一击，七个毙命”八个大字，以为是他一下打死了七个人，心底不禁对这个小家伙生出一丝敬意。不过，他还是想先考考小裁缝。他捡起一块石头，放在手中用力一握，就有水从石头里滴下来。“你也来一个，”巨人说，“你不是力大无比么？”“就这个呀？”小裁缝说，“这个连三岁小孩儿都会！”他把手伸入口袋，拿出那块奶酪，用力握在手里，就有水从奶酪里流出来了。“你不得不承认，”小裁缝说，“我比你强吧？”巨人一时不知道说什么好了，他无法相信小裁缝如此瘦小的身躯里面怎么会蕴藏着这么大的力量。他从地上捡起一块石头，把它抛到空中，石头飞得很高，人眼几乎都看不见了。“小不点儿，你也做一个让我瞧瞧。”“干得漂亮，”小裁缝说道，“不过，毕竟你的石头又落地了，我就让你开开眼界，因为我扔的石头永远都不会落到地上。”他从口袋里拿出那只小鸟，把它抛入空中。小鸟重获自由，激动地飞向高空，飞向远方，再也没有回来。“朋友，这下你该认输了吧？”小裁缝问道。“看来你扔东西确实很在行，”巨人说道，“不过现在让我瞧瞧你搬东西如何。”他把小裁缝带到一棵倒在地上的大橡树旁，说：“如果你够强壮的话，就帮我把这棵树搬出这片森林吧。”“乐意之至，”小裁缝说，“你把树干扛到肩上，最重的树枝交给我就行了。”巨人把树干扛到肩上，小裁缝舒舒服服地坐在后面的一个树枝上。巨人一个人扛着整棵树和小裁缝，无法回头。快乐的小裁缝在他身后唱起歌来：“三个裁缝离开从大门口骑马出发......”好像搬运大树对他来说时小孩子的把戏。巨人拖着重负走到半路，再也坚持不下去了，叫道：“听着！我要把树放下来了！”小裁缝一听，迅速从树枝上跳了下来，双手抱起树干，做出一副好像一直都在搬树的样子，对巨人喊道：“你身材如此魁梧，却连一棵树也搬不动！” “Oh, indeed?” answered the little tailor, and unbuttoned his coat, and showed the giant the girdle, “There mayst thou read what kind of a man I am!”The giant read, “Seven at one stroke,” and thought that they had been men whom the tailor had killed, and began to feel a little respect for the tiny fellow.Nevertheless, he wished to try him first, and took a stone in his hand and squeezed it together so that water dropped out of it.“Do that likewise,” said the giant, “if thou hast strength?”“Is that all?” said the tailor, “that is child’s play with us!” and put his hand into his pocket, brought out the soft cheese, and pressed it until the liquid ran out of it.“Faith,” said he, “that was a little better, wasn’t it?”The giant did not know what to say, and could not believe it of the little man.Then the giant picked up a stone and threw it so high that the eye could scarcely follow it.“Now, little mite of a man, do that likewise.”“Well thrown,” said the tailor, “but after all the stone came down to earth again; I will throw you one which shall never come back at all.”And he put his hand into his pocket, took out the bird, and threw it into the air.The bird, delighted with its liberty, rose, flew away and did not come back.“How does that shot please you, comrade?” asked the tailor.“Thou canst certainly throw,” said the giant, “but now we will see if thou art able to carry anything properly.”He took the little tailor to a mighty oak tree which lay there felled on the ground, and said, “If thou art strong enough, help me to carry the tree out of the forest.”“Readily,” answered the little man; “take thou the trunk on thy shoulders, and I will raise up the branches and twigs; after all, they are the heaviest.”The giant took the trunk on his shoulder, but the tailor seated himself on a branch, and the giant who could not look round, had to carry away the whole tree, and the little tailor into the bargain: he behind, was quite merry and happy, and whistled the song, “Three tailors rode forth from the gate,” as if carrying the tree were child’s play.The giant, after he had dragged the heavy burden part of the way, could go no further, and cried, “Hark you, I shall have to let the tree fall!”The tailor sprang nimbly down, seized the tree with both arms as if he had been carrying it, and said to the giant, “Thou art such a great fellow, and yet canst not even carry the tree!”
他们继续往前走。路经一棵樱桃树时，巨人伸手抓住最高的那个树枝，枝头结满了熟透了的樱桃。他拽下树枝，递到小裁缝手中，让他吃些樱桃。小裁缝接过树枝，巨人就把手松开了。弱小的小裁缝怎么可能有力气拽住树枝，树枝弹回去了，一下子把他弹到了空中。他跌在地上，倒也毫发无伤。巨人问：“怎么回事？你怎么这么没力气，连这么细的树枝都拽不住？”“这跟力气没有关系，”小裁缝答道，“我一下能打死七个呢，这个对我来说算得了什么？因为我刚才看到有猎人藏在灌木丛中打枪，所以就从树上跳了过去。要不你也照我的样子，看看能不能跳过去。”巨人试着跳过樱桃树，可是始终无法做到，最后反而挂在了树枝上。这下小裁缝又占了上风。 They went on together, and as they passed a cherry-tree, the giant laid hold of the top of the tree where the ripest fruit was hanging, bent it down, gave it into the tailor’s hand, and bade him eat.But the little tailor was much too weak to hold the tree, and when the giant let it go, it sprang back again, and the tailor was hurried into the air with it.When he had fallen down again without injury, the giant said, “What is this?Hast thou not strength enough to hold the weak twig?”“There is no lack of strength,” answered the little tailor.“Dost thou think that could be anything to a man who has struck down seven at one blow?I leapt over the tree because the huntsmen are shooting down there in the thicket.Jump as I did, if thou canst do it.”The giant made the attempt, but could not get over the tree, and remained hanging in the branches, so that in this also the tailor kept the upper hand.
巨人说道：“既然你有如此胆量，何不随我一起到我们居住的山洞里过夜呢？”小裁缝欣然接受了巨人的邀请，随他来到了巨人居住的山洞。他们走进山洞时，其他巨人正围坐在火边，每人手里攥着一只烤羊，正在大快朵颐。小裁缝环顾四周，想：“这里可比我的裁缝铺宽敞多了。”巨人把小裁缝带到一个床铺前，说他今晚可以睡在那里。然而，这个床铺对小裁缝来说实在太大了，所以他没有睡在床上，而是蜷缩在一个角落里休息。午夜时分，巨人估计小裁缝肯定已经躺在床上睡熟了，就悄悄起床，手持一根铁条，一下把小裁缝的床劈成两半，以为这下小裁缝肯定完蛋了。天一亮巨人们就去了森林，把小裁缝忘得一干二净。突然，小裁缝兴冲冲地走到他们面前，胆气十足。巨人们心惊胆战，唯恐小裁缝把他们全都打死，慌忙抱头鼠窜。 The giant said, “If thou art such a valiant fellow, come with me into our cavern and spend the night with us.”The little tailor was willing, and followed him.When they went into the cave, other giants were sitting there by the fire, and each of them had a roasted sheep in his hand and was eating it.The little tailor looked round and thought, “It is much more spacious here than in my workshop.”The giant showed him a bed, and said he was to lie down in it and sleep.The bed, however, was too big for the little tailor; he did not lie down in it, but crept into a corner.When it was midnight, and the giant thought that the little tailor was lying in a sound sleep, he got up, took a great iron bar, cut through the bed with one blow, and thought he had given the grasshopper his finishing stroke.With the earliest dawn the giants went into the forest, and had quite forgotten the little tailor, when all at once he walked up to them quite merrily and boldly.The giants were terrified, they were afraid that he would strike them all dead, and ran away in a great hurry.
小裁缝再次踏上征程，一路勇往直前。他走了很久后，来到了一座宫殿的庭院里。因为他感到非常疲倦，就躺在草地上睡着了。他躺在那里睡觉的时候，众人走过来，围着他上下打量，然后看到了他腰带上面的字：轻轻一击，七个毙命。他们说：“咦，我们这里一片和平，没有战争，为什么这位勇士会出现在这里？他肯定是位了不起的贵族。”他们把这件事情禀告给国王，并且劝告国王如果爆发战争的话，这个厉害的角色就可以派上用场，所以无论如何都要把他留下来。这个建议正中国王下怀。他派出一名大臣，等小裁缝睡醒后，就劝他留在军中服役。这位奉了使命的大臣静静地守候在熟睡的小裁缝身边。直到他伸了伸懒腰，睁开双眼，大臣才向他传达了国王的旨意。“我正是为了这个才来到这里的，”小裁缝回复道，“我乐意为国王效劳。”小裁缝受到了贵宾般的款待，国王还赏赐了他一处气派的住所。 The little tailor went onwards, always following his own pointed nose.After he had walked for a long time, he came to the courtyard of a royal palace, and as he felt weary, he lay down on the grass and fell asleep.Whilst he lay there, the people came and inspected him on all sides, and read on his girdle, “Seven at one stroke.”“Ah,” said they, “What does the great warrior here in the midst of peace?He must be a mighty lord.”They went and announced him to the King, and gave it as their opinion that if war should break out, this would be a weighty and useful man who ought on no account to be allowed to depart.The counsel pleased the King, and he sent one of his courtiers to the little tailor to offer him military service when he awoke.The ambassador remained standing by the sleeper, waited until he stretched his limbs and opened his eyes, and then conveyed to him this proposal.“For this very reason have I come here,” the tailor replied, “I am ready to enter the King’s service.”He was therefore honorably received and a special dwelling was assigned him.
然而，士兵们都很不喜欢小裁缝，希望他走得越远越好。“怎么才能让他离开呢？”他们在一起议论道。“如果我们和他发生争执，他发起狠来，一下就能打死七个，我们中没有一个人可以与他匹敌。”他们最终决定一起去面见国王，恳请国王允许他们退役。他们对国王说道：“我们没有办法和一个‘一击之下，七个毙命’的人共事。”国王不愿为了小裁缝一个人失去所有忠诚的奴仆。他想自己若是从来没看到过小裁缝该多好，又希望小裁缝能赶紧离开。然而，他不敢对小裁缝直言让他离开，因为他也害怕小裁缝一气之下把自己和国民都杀死，然后自己登上王位。他思索良久，终于想到了一个好主意。他派人通告小裁缝，他有一件事情需要他这个伟大的勇士去做。在这个国家的一片森林里，有两个巨人烧杀抢掠，无恶不作，危害一方，普通人接近他们无疑是羊入虎口。国王许诺小裁缝，如果他能战胜并除掉这两个巨人，他就把自己唯一的女儿嫁给他，还会把国土的一半作为女儿的嫁妆赠予他。除此之外，国王还派一百名骑兵协助他。“这对我这样的人来说确实是再好不过的事情了。”小裁缝在心里盘算着，“一个美丽的公主和一半的国土，这样的好事不是每天都能碰到的！”“遵命，”小裁缝回复国王，“不过我并不需要一百名骑兵的协助，我一个人就能很快制服巨人。对我来说，杀死七个都易如反掌，更何况是两个呢？” The soldiers, however, were set against the little tailor, and wished him a thousand miles away.“What is to be the end of this?” they said amongst themselves.“If we quarrel with him, and he strikes about him, seven of us will fall at every blow; not one of us can stand against him.”They came therefore to a decision, betook themselves in a body to the King, and begged for their dismissal.“We are not prepared,” said they, “to stay with a man who kills seven at one stroke.”The King was sorry that for the sake of one he should lose all his faithful servants, wished that he had never set eyes on the tailor, and would willingly have been rid of him again.But he did not venture to give him his dismissal, for he dreaded lest he should strike him and all his people dead, and place himself on the royal throne.He thought about it for a long time, and at last found good counsel.He sent to the little tailor and caused him to be informed that as he was such a great warrior, he had one request to make to him.In a forest of his country lived two giants who caused great mischief with their robbing, murdering, ravaging, and burning, and no one could approach them without putting himself in danger of death.If the tailor conquered and killed these two giants, he would give him his only daughter to wife, and half of his kingdom as a dowry, likewise one hundred horsemen should go with him to assist him.“That would indeed be a fine thing for a man like me!” thought the little tailor.“One is not offered a beautiful princess and half a kingdom every day of one’s life!”“Oh, yes,” he replied, “I will soon subdue the giants, and do not require the help of the hundred horsemen to do it; he who can hit seven with one blow has no need to be afraid of two.”
小裁缝出发了，那一百名骑兵还是跟在他的身后。到达森林边缘时，他对随行的那一百名骑兵说道：“你们就在这里等着，我很快就会干掉那两个巨人。”说完他就钻进森林，四处搜寻。不一会儿他就找到了那两个巨人。他们正躺在一棵树下睡觉，鼾声如雷，树枝都被震得上下起伏。小裁缝可没闲着，他捡了满满两个口袋的石子，爬到了树上。爬到一半的时候，他顺着一个树枝滑到半空，正好坐在巨人的上方。他把石子一个接一个扔到其中一个巨人的胸口上。很长一段时间里，那个巨人都丝毫没有察觉。不过最终他还是醒了过来，推了推同伴，说：“你打我干吗？”“你是在做梦吧，”他的同伴说，“我没有打你啊！”他们又躺了下来，准备继续睡觉。小裁缝拿石子朝第二个巨人扔去。“你这是什么意思？”第二个巨人吼道，“你干吗打我？”“我没有打你。”第一个巨人吼道。他们争吵了一会儿，不过因为实在太困了，就停止了争吵，再次合上了眼睛。小裁缝又故伎重演，挑出最大的石头，用尽全力朝第一个巨人的胸部砸去。“太过分了！”第一个巨人吼道，疯了一般从地上跳起来，把同伴往树上推去，树都被震得不住地摇晃。另一个巨人立马还击，两个巨人顿时扭作一团，如同疯了一样，树都在他们的恶斗中被连根拔起。他们撕打了很长时间，最后两个巨人双双倒地而亡。这时，小裁缝才跳下树来。他自言自语：“幸亏他们没有把我藏身的这棵树连根拔起，真是走运。要不然的话，我就只有像松鼠一样跳到另外一棵树上去了。不过还好，我们这些裁缝个个都身手敏捷。”他拔出宝剑，在两个巨人的胸口上各刺了几剑，然后走出森林，找到骑兵，对他们说道：“事情办好了，我已经把那两个巨人全部干掉了，不过，还真挺不容易的呢！他们跟我对打的时候，为了活命，竟然把大树连根拔起用作防御。不过，这对我来说可起不了什么作用，我一击就能致七个毙命呢！”“难道你一点儿都没受伤吗？”骑兵问道。“你多虑啦，”小裁缝说，“他们连我一根毫毛都没伤着。”骑兵对小裁缝说的话半信半疑，就骑马进入森林查看。他们看到两个巨人倒在血泊之中，横在他们尸体旁边的正是连根拔起的大树。 The little tailor went forth, and the hundred horsemen followed him.When he came to the outskirts of the forest, he said to his followers, “Just stay waiting here, I alone will soon finish off the giants.”Then he bounded into the forest and looked about right and left.After a while he perceived both giants.They lay sleeping under a tree, and snored so that the branches waved up and down.The little tailor, not idle, gathered two pocketsful of stones, and with these climbed up the tree.When he was half-way up, he slipped down by a branch, until he sat just above the sleepers, and then let one stone after another fall on the breast of one of the giants.For a long time the giant felt nothing, but at last he awoke, pushed his comrade, and said, “Why art thou knocking me?”“Thou must be dreaming,” said the other, “I am not knocking thee.”They laid themselves down to sleep again, and then the tailor threw a stone down on the second.“What is the meaning of this?” cried the other.“Why art thou pelting me?”“I am not pelting thee,” answered the first, growling.They disputed about it for a time, but as they were weary they let the matter rest, and their eyes closed once more.The little tailor began his game again, picked out the biggest stone, and threw it with all his might on the breast of the first giant.“That is too bad!” cried he, and sprang up like a madman, and pushed his companion against the tree until it shook.The other paid him back in the same coin, and they got into such a rage that they tore up trees and belabored each other so long, that at last they both fell down dead on the ground at the same time.Then the little tailor leapt down.“It is a lucky thing,” said he, “that they did not tear up the tree on which I was sitting, or I should have had to spring on to another like a squirrel; but we tailors are nimble.”He drew out his sword and gave each of them a couple of thrusts in the breast, and then went out to the horsemen and said, “The work is done; I have given both of them their finishing stroke, but it was hard work!They tore up trees in their sore need, and defended themselves with them, but all that is to no purpose when a man like myself comes, who can kill seven at one blow.”“But are you not wounded?” asked the horsemen.“You need not concern yourself about that,” answered the tailor, “They have not bent one hair of mine.”The horsemen would not believe him, and rode into the forest; there they found the giants swimming in their blood, and all round about lay the torn-up trees.
小裁缝请求国王兑现自己的诺言。然而，国王已经反悔了，他又开始思索赶走这个大英雄的计策了。“在你迎娶我的女儿、接受一半的国土之前，”他对小裁缝说道，“你必须完成一件壮举。在森林里，有一只独角兽出没，危害一方，你必须首先把它制服。”“恐怕一只独角兽比两个巨人容易对付多了。我一击之下能致七个毙命。”他带了一根绳子和一把斧头就只身走进森林，让那些国王派来协助他的人在外面等候。他找了很久。独角兽终于出现了，它走过来，二话不说就向小裁缝扑去，欲用其锋利的兽角把小裁缝剖成碎片。“别着急，别着急，这么急躁可不行。”小裁缝说道。他站在那里一动不动，直到眼看独角兽就要扑到自己身上了，才敏捷地窜到树后面去。独角兽竭尽全力撞向小裁缝身前的大树，兽角深深地扎入树干，怎么也拔不出来了。它就这样被擒了。“现在，猎物终于落网了。”小裁缝从树后走出来，把绳子绕在独角兽的脖子上绑好，又用斧头把它陷在树干中的角砍断。一切都办妥当之后，他带着独角兽去面见国王。 The little tailor demanded of the King the promised reward; he, however, repented of his promise, and again bethought himself how he could get rid of the hero.“Before thou receivest my daughter, and the half of my kingdom,” said he to him, “thou must perform one more heroic deed.In the forest roams a unicorn which does great harm, and thou must catch it first.”“I fear one unicorn still less than two giants.Seven at one blow, is my kind of affair.”He took a rope and an axe with him, went forth into the forest, and again bade those who were sent with him to wait outside.He had to seek long.The unicorn soon came towards him, and rushed directly on the tailor, as if it would spit him on his horn without more ceremony.“Softly, softly; it can’t be done as quickly as that,” said he, and stood still and waited until the animal was quite close, and then sprang nimbly behind the tree.The unicorn ran against the tree with all its strength, and struck its horn so fast in the trunk that it had not strength enough to draw it out again, and thus it was caught.“Now, I have got the bird,” said the tailor, and came out from behind the tree and put the rope round its neck, and then with his axe he hewed the horn out of the tree, and when all was ready he led the beast away and took it to the King.
国王还是不想兑现自己的诺言，又给了小裁缝第三个使命。在小裁缝和公主举行婚礼之前，他必须擒住一只在森林里作乱的野猪，猎人们会给予他帮助。“没问题，”小裁缝说，“小菜一碟！”小裁缝又让猎人们在外面等候，只身进入森林。猎人们对这个安排求之不得。他们多次见识过这头野猪的厉害，根本不想进去埋伏了。野猪发现小裁缝后，立马流着口水、张开利齿向小裁缝扑去，试图把他扑倒在地。然而，身手敏捷的小裁缝一下钻进了附近的一个教堂，爬上窗户，而后又一跃跳了出来。野猪一路追进了教堂，然而小裁缝这时已在外面，他绕到教堂门口，把门给锁上了。这头狂怒的野猪试图从窗户里跳出去，可惜身躯过于笨重，怎么也跳不出去，只好乖乖就擒。小裁缝把候在外面的猎人叫过来，让他们亲眼看看被擒的野兽。大英雄去面见国王，国王别无他法，只好遵照承诺，把公主和一半的国土赠予他。如果他知道此刻站在面前的根本不是什么勇于作战的大英雄，而只是一个小裁缝的话，估计他会比这时更加伤心难过。婚礼终于举行了，隆重却没有一点欢乐的气氛。小裁缝竟然成了国王。 The King still would not give him the promised reward, and made a third demand.Before the wedding the tailor was to catch him a wild boar that made great havoc in the forest, and the huntsmen should give him their help.“Willingly,” said the tailor, “that is child’s play!”He did not take the huntsmen with him into the forest, and they were well pleased that he did not, for the wild boar had several times received them in such a manner that they had no inclination to lie in wait for him.When the boar perceived the tailor, it ran on him with foaming mouth and whetted tusks, and was about to throw him to the ground, but the active hero sprang into a chapel which was near, and up to the window at once, and in one bound out again.The boar ran in after him, but the tailor ran round outside and shut the door behind it, and then the raging beast, which was much too heavy and awkward to leap out of the window, was caught.The little tailor called the huntsmen thither that they might see the prisoner with their own eyes.The hero, however went to the King, who was now, whether he liked it or not, obliged to keep his promise, and gave him his daughter and the half of his kingdom.Had he known that it was no warlike hero, but a little tailor who was standing before him, it would have gone to his heart still more than it did.The wedding was held with great magnificence and small joy, and out of a tailor a king was made.
过了一段时间，年轻的王后听到丈夫在夜里说梦话：“小子，给我做一件紧身上衣，还要把我的马裤补好，否则我就用尺子敲你的耳朵。”就这样，她发现了这个年轻君主的出身。第二天一早，她就跑到国王那里抱怨自己悲惨的命运，请求他除掉她那原本不过是个小裁缝的丈夫。国王安慰了她一番，说：“今天晚上不要锁卧室门，我的仆人会守在外面。等他睡着后，他们就会进去把他捆起来，带到一艘船上去，让他顺水漂走。”公主听了父王的话满心欢喜。不过，他们的对话被国王的一个侍卫听到了，他和年轻的国王很要好，就把这件事原原本本告诉了他。“我自有办法。”小裁缝说道。这天晚上他跟妻子像往常一样上床歇息，过了一会，公主以为小裁缝已经睡熟了，就起身去把卧室门打开，然后又回去躺好。小裁缝假装已经睡熟，装作说梦话，清晰地喊道：“小子，给我做一件紧身上衣，还要把我的马裤补好，否则我就用尺子敲你的耳朵。我一击之下能致七个毙命。我杀死了两个巨人，制服了一头独角兽，还活捉了一头野猪，我会惧怕那些站在屋外的人吗？”守在屋外的人听到小裁缝的话，深感恐惧，抱头鼠窜，好像有疯狂的猎人在后面追赶他们似的。再也没有一个人胆敢与他作对了。于是，小裁缝登上了国王的宝座，直到终老。 After some time the young Queen heard her husband say in his dreams at night, “Boy, make me the doublet, and patch the pantaloons, or else I will rap the yard-measure over thine ears.”Then she discovered in what state of life the young lord had been born, and next morning complained of her wrongs to her father, and begged him to help her to get rid of her husband, who was nothing else but a tailor.The King comforted her and said, “Leave thy bed-room door open this night, and my servants shall stand outside, and when he has fallen asleep shall go in, bind him, and take him on board a ship which shall carry him into the wide world.”The woman was satisfied with this; but the King’s armour-bearer, who had heard all, was friendly with the young lord, and informed him of the whole plot.“I’ll put a screw into that business,” said the little tailor.At night he went to bed with his wife at the usual time, and when she thought that he had fallen asleep, she got up, opened the door, and then lay down again.The little tailor, who was only pretending to be asleep, began to cry out in a clear voice, “Boy, make me the doublet and patch me the pantaloons, or I will rap the yard-measure over thine ears.I smote seven at one blow.I killed two giants, I brought away one unicorn and caught a wild boar, and am I to fear those who are standing outside the room.”When these men heard the tailor speaking thus, they were overcome by a great dread, and ran as if the wild huntsman were behind them, and none of them would venture anything further against him.So the little tailor was a king and remained one, to the end of his life.
活命水 The Water of Life
